PREFACE
Back in the summers of 1911 and 1912, Marius Barbeau came to Oklahoma to research
the Wandat1 language. He worked with many speakers, including Catherine Johnson, Smith
Nichols, Star Young, John Kayrahoo, Allen Johnson, Eldredge Brown, Mary Kelley, Henry
Stand, and Mary McKee (of Anderdon). One of the results of their labors was the
Traditional Narratives, published in 1960, a collection of approximately 253 pages of
legible handwritten transcription of Wandat and typed word glosses, with an additional 51
typed pages of free translation, totaling over 12,700 words.
Although others had researched the Wandat language before him (Connelley, Hewitt,
Haldeman, Gatschet, etc.), Barbeau is the one who left the bulk of resources available to
us. Compare Barbeau’s 253 pages of text with the texts others produced: four or so
versions of the Lord’s Prayer (one page each), the Grey-Eyes speech (four pages), the
untranslated hymns of Armstrong and Brown (63 pages). Barbeau’s texts are longer than
all the others combined!
This edition of the Traditional Narratives has been produced in honor of the 100th
anniversary of Barbeau’s fieldwork, with ease of use kept in mind. Instead of a full linguistic
analysis, this version sticks close to what Barbeau himself produced, albeit reorganized and
regularized for clarity, with the inclusion of a regularized spelling. Additionally, corrections
have been made to word boundaries. The notebook format is to allow students to more
easily work with the texts, such as letting the book lie flat while a student takes notes.
Furthermore, corrections are easily handled with replacement of modified pages. The
notebook can be easily extended to handle a full analysis.
Format
The original format used by Barbeau included only Wandat words and word glosses,
arranged in parallel columns, rather than interlinearly. Words and glosses were co-indexed
by numbers. Free translations were placed in a separate section, preceding the parallel
texts, and indexed with them by title and text number. These translations are almost
identical to the English texts found in Barbeau’s Huron and Wyandot Mythology, published
in 1915. To reduce the amount of flipping back and forth required by the original, this
edition uses an interlinear format, where all the information is lined up, as seen in the
following example:
TN:10:106:40-43
aʔú:dinǫ̀ katu déʔkaʔ dutù:wéhtęʔ
a^>u'.8divno;` katu^ dE'>ka> 8du^tu`.we'<tE;>
she.fell.into.a.trance there then that.it.(to).her.appeared.[the.otter]
A woman [of the Big Turtle Clan] fell into a trance. The White Otter
appeared to her,
6
1 The name of the language has been spelled many ways over the years, including Wyandot,
Wyandott, Wyandotte, Wandót, Wandat, Wanat, and Junúndat. In the language itself, the name is
Waⁿdat, so although “Wyandot” is the most common spelling, we will use Wandat here.
The first line is a simple code indicating exactly where the source words can be found in
Barbeau’s original. TN indicates Traditional Narratives, while the number after the colon is
the text number. In this case, we are looking at the beginning of text #10, The White
Otter. The second number, after another colon, is the original page number, while the last
number or range of numbers gives the original word number. This way, anyone can look
up the line in a copy of the original Traditional Narratives and verify accuracy.
The second line is a modern regularized spelling, avoiding many of the extraneous details
recorded by Barbeau. The third line is Barbeau’s original, typed instead of handwritten. The
orthography used is explained elsewhere.
The fourth line gives the word-by-word translations, as given by Barbeau. Whereas the
original separated the Wandat and English, but co-indexed them with numbers, here they
are aligned above each other.
Fifth or more lines are the free translation in more flowing English. These are the
translations as given by Barbeau.
Difficulties appeared in preparing these lines in that there were frequently discrepancies
between numbering, glossing, and writing of words. A string of text might be written by
Barbeau as two words, but glossed as one; written as one word, but numbered as two; or
any combination of the three factors.
For instance, in text #21, The Boy and His Pet Snake, the word for again he goes out
hunting appears both as a single word:2
TN:21:152:04
sahu^nE;'.ro;^tI
again.he.goes.out.hunting
and as two separate words:
TN:21:151:46-47
sa hu^nE;'.ro;^tI>
again:he.goes.out.hunting
The original lines have been corrected to match a modern understanding of the word
structure. In other words, where Barbeau split a single word into multiple parts, here they
are put back together. Where Barbeau joined separate words, here they are split up.
Although generally fairly legible, in the original handwriting word boundaries were not
always clear. That is, the amount of space between words was sometimes non-existent.
Furthermore, certain characters, including < > and ., were difficult to distinguish from each
other.
7
2 Periods are inserted in the glosses as a way to indicate that the parts belong to the same gloss. A
colon indicates that Barbeau split the gloss into two or more separate glosses.
In many cases, different ordering of elements between English and Wandat forced glosses
and translations to be on separate lines, so that there is not always a free translation line,
or there may be multiple free translation lines.
Within Barbeau’s texts there is little overt indication of structure in the sense of no
convenient indicators of sentence or clause boundaries, such as punctuation, particular
physical layouts, intonation contours, or numbering of items. Lines of text are physical
rather than linguistic in nature. That is, a line does not indicate an utterance unit of some
sort, but only the amount of text that could be written across a page.
On the other hand, three potential levels of organization are discernible. At the top level is
the entire text itself, always distinct from other texts. A text can run anywhere from only a
single page (e.g. #10, The White Otter) to 25 pages in length (e.g. #27, The Steer and the
Ill-Treated Stepson). At the bottom level is the word.
The intermediary level is the least certain. There are occasional indentations at the
beginnings of lines that give the appearance of paragraphing. Their purpose is not
explained by Barbeau. Sometimes such an indent occurs where one would expect a
paragraph break in English, but the correlations are inconsistent. A Wandat indent might or
might not occur where an English break would be, and an English break might or might
not occur where a Wandat indent appears. A block of text separated by indents might be
only a single word, or cover multiple pages. Additionally, on rare occasions a large amount
of white space appears within a line, giving the visual impression of a break.
Thus, there are no readily usable indications of what a Wandat speaker might think of as a
sentence or clause. This is further complicated by the fact that, due to Wandat’s
polysynthetic nature, even a single word can contain all the elements necessary for a
complete utterance.
Orthography
Barbeau’s original spelling system is reproduced here so that readers can see the original,
but it is superseded by a regularized and modernized spelling. An explanation of the
original can be found in many of Barbeau’s publications, including of course the original
Traditional Narratives.
Why change? There are several difficulties in using Barbeau’s orthographic system, one of
which is the use of non-standard characters. For instance, k, represents the string ky, while <
and > stand for h and ʔ (glottal stop). This is problematic for reasons of interpretation, in
that there is no accepted standard for the characters, as there is for modern standards, for
instance. Furthermore, < and > can be difficult to distinguish in handwriting. Typographically,
obscure symbols are harder to access for publication purposes. Indeed, to first do work
with the Wandat language, I had to obtain a font creation program in order to create
letters that to this day do not exist in Unicode (which did not exist at the time). The
Barbeau spelling here was produced with the result, a TrueType font named barbeau.ttf. A
further difficulty is that there is an abundance of stackable diacritics reducing ease of
reading. A vowel can have a nasal hook underneath, a breve above, and an acute or
8
grave above that! The detail of Barbeau’s orthography is both a blessing and a curse. The
amount of detail is staggering, and very useful for recovering exact pronunciation. On the
other hand, Barbeau recorded every detail he could, even when those details were
redundant or spontaneous. If we used the same level of detail for English, we would have
to write pun and spun with different p’s, because they are actually pronounced differently
(pun is aspirated, spun is not); we would have to write spun and spud with different u’s
(spun is nasalized, spud is not); and we would have to write the u’s of spun and spud
differently from sputter (spun and spud are long, sputter is short). We would sometimes
write something as somethin, sometimes as sumpin.
The Wandat word for he said shows up with at least 22 different spellings in the
Traditional Narratives:
ahEhao;'>
ahE;`hao;'>
ahE;`ha^o;'>
a^hE;`hao;'>
a^hE;`ha^o;'>
ahE;`8ha^o;'>
ahE;hao;'>
a^hE;hao;'>
a^hE^\ha;^o;'>
ahE^ha;'o;>
ahE;`ha;o;'>
a^hE;ha;o;>
ahE;ha;o;'>
ahE;`ha;^o;'>
a`hE;ha;o;'>
a^hE;ha;o;'>
a^hE;`ha;o;'>
a^hE;`ha;^o;'>
a^hE;^ha;^o;'>
ahE;ha;.o;'>
a;hE;`ha;^o;'>
a;^hE;`ha;^o;'>
And these do not include alternate translations, such as he says, he answered, etc. In the
modern regularized spelling, these all become ahęhaǫ́ʔ, which is much easier to read.
9
Consonant symbols used
In the regularized spelling, these letters have the following consonant sounds:
d as in English undone. A faint n (prenasalization) can be freely added or not to the
beginning, ⁿd
g as in English ingrown. A faint n (prenasalization) can be freely added or not to the
beginning ⁿg
h as in English Hun. In Wandat, h can be at the ends of words or before consonants,
unlike English
k as in English cut
m as in English moon
n as in English none
r as in English run. rh can be very retroflexed
s as in English sun
š as in English ship
t as in English ton
w as in English won
y as in English yes
ž as in English measure
ʔ as in English uh-oh. The glottal stop shows up in English interjections, but not
content words
Note that this is regularization rather than phonemicization, to aid reading by English
speakers. In a phonemic system, m would simply be w near a nasal vowel and g would
be d before a glide.
Vowel symbols used
a as in English father
ą as a but nasalized, font
e as in English mate or met
ę as in English mental, nasalized
i as in English feet or fit
ǫ as in English owned, nasalized
u as in English rule
In addition, length of vowels is indicated by a colon (:). Note that length is used literally,
that is the vowel is pronounced for a longer period of time, and not in the English
grammar sense of, for example, long i versus short i, which are different vowel sounds
altogether.
Primary stress, a high or rising pitch, is indicated by an acute accent ( ́), while secondary
stress is indicated by a grave accent ( ̀).
The difference between oral and nasal vowels, as seen in the list, is marked by a nasal
hook or ogonek ( ̨).
10
A Personal Note
I have often been asked what led me to study the Wandat language. That’s not an easy
question to answer! I grew up in a town once known to Iroquoian speakers as
Canestigione, referring to corn flats, and I went to a school where almost all buildings on
the campus had names from various Iroquoian languages. We were taught in elementary
school the story of the woman who fell from the sky. From the very beginning I knew I
lived in old Iroquoia.
When I discovered linguistics in grad school, I entered a department at a university with a
tradition of researching Iroquoian languages. Of course, I focused on these languages
starting my first semester. Over the years of my training, I researched all of the Iroquoian
languages, culminating in my dissertation, a grammar of Wandat.
Dr. Craig Kopris
July, 2012

TN:07:100:34-36

two sides of the fire] were his cousins. Now then, the four clans on one side
of the fire formed ‘one house,’ while the four opposite clans made up
another. As to the Wolf, he remained all by himself. The four clans in each
house were ‘brothers’ to one another. Were all [mutual] ‘cousins’ [those who
belonged to opposite houses, so that whoever stood on one side of the fire
called anybody standing on the other side ‘cousin.’] It was then agreed that
this was the law the Wyandots must observe. And it had to be proclaimed in
such a way that on all possible occasions the opposite groups should be known
as ‘cousins.’

TN:15:125:19-26
kihuwéręʔ ne hažáhšaʔ sayuhǫhšrę́:mah
kivhuwe^/rE;> ne ha^ja'<ca> sayu^ho;<crE'.ma;<
he.held.up the his.arm back.the.board.he.had
Then they began talking together. “You are extremely small!” said he. “You must
be very young?” But she retorted, “No! I am really much older than you are."
He could not believe her as she was so very small. Then they talked about
things of the past, things that had happened long ago. She told him all about
events long since forgotten, things which he had never heard of. She truly
thought herself to be much older than he, but he would not believe it. “Do you
remember the time,” asked she, “when this earth was drowned?” And he inquired
where the people went while the earth was covered with water. She explained
that they climbed up into the cliffs. He was, in fact, so very much taller than
she, that he could not help believing himself the older. “Were you living,”
said he, “at the time when the rocks became rotten?” She did not know and had
never heard anything about it. He asked her, then, whether she had ever sene
any of the rocks that had holes inside and were dreadful to look at. She had
seen such rocks. He added that they were the rocks that had become rotten. Now
she believed him and thought him the older, for during all her lifetime she had
never seen a rock that had become rotten. The hunter then put the little
woman’s child into his pocket. She wanted it so badly, however, that he pulled
it out and offered it to her. As she was just about to get hold of it, he
withdrew his hand quickly. Again he held the child high over his head, so that
she could not possibly reach it. She took a leap and grabbed his arm, but he
shifted the cradle-board into his other hand. She leaped again and pulled his
other arm down, but to no avail as he was now holding the child in his other
hand. The same thing happened three times when again the Tiken’an grabbed the
hunter’s arm. Wu’! It was now bent awfully at the elbow. She exclaimed, “I
have broken your arm!” For, it is well known that the arms of these little
folks have joints only at the shoulder and the wrist, and none at the elbow.
She had, moreover, never seen anybody bending his arm at the elbow, just like
this. So she was sure that she had broken his arm.
129

Place: Wyandotte, Oklahoma
Date: July 1912
Informant: Catherine Johnson
Translator: Allen Johnson
Cf: Huron and Wyandot Mythology 137-138
Note: Mrs. Johnson stated that she learned this tale from her grandmother, Nenduca, of the Deer clan

316
TAWIDI’A AND HIS UNCLE
Place: Wyandotte, Oklahoma
Date: 1911-12
Informant: Catherine Johnson
Interpreter: Mary Kelley and Eldredge Brown; Allen Johnson
Cf: Huron and Wyandot Mythology 224-232
Note: In November 1911, the first part (that is, up to the point where the uncle sends Tawidi'a to
invite guests to the feast) was taken down in English through the interpreters Mary Kelley and
Eldredge Brown (chiefly the latter). The work of the interpreters proved so unsatisfactory, on account
of the inherent intricacies, that it was found more practical to take down the balance of the tale in
Wyandot text. This part of the text was interpreted by E. Brown and M. Kelley. In April 1912, the
text for the first part was completed by Catherine Johnson and interpreted by Mary Kelley. Later, the
whole text was revised and reinterpreted with the help of Allen Johnson.

Tawidi’a shot [an arrow] through the finger. The old man spat on his finger
and rubbed the wound on his side, at once healing both finger and side at the

bear, this time with his protruded lips. Tawidi’a shot an arrow through his
uncle’s lips.

nahúʔšrǫtahs daè hą́ʔrą ne hutuyę́ʔah
na^hu'>7cro;^8ta<s daE` ha;'>1ra; nE^ hutu^yE;'>a;<
the:his.axe.sets.by.the.fire that.one.(just) only the he.axe.owns
and flint axe, together with some small wooden balls that he had just made
out of twigs. He had again misunderstood the old man [who had given him these
directions in the old language].
*When his uncle used yikara, an old word for “loins,” his nephew understood
‘korya, breech-cloth.*
*Instead of ya’cra, tallow, he heard a’cra, axe.*
*Instead of kwe’ta, balls of roasted corn, he understood ya’kwe’ta, little
balls of wood.*
*Instead of a utuyen, the tallow, tu’yen, axe.*
